<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17612365159426462530"><em>If I could tell you only one thing about my life it would be this: when I was seven years old the mailman ran over my head. As formative events go, nothing else comes close</em>. <br /><br />With these words Edgar Mint, half-Apache and mostly orphaned, makes his unshakable claim on our attention. In the course of Brady Udall’s high-spirited, inexhaustibly inventive novel, Edgar survives no</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17612365159426462530"><em>If I could tell you only one thing about my life it would be this: when I was seven years old the mailman ran over my head. As formative events go, nothing else comes close</em>. <br /><br />With these words Edgar Mint, half-Apache and mostly orphaned, makes his unshakable claim on our attention. In the course of Brady Udall’s high-spirited, inexhaustibly inventive novel, Edgar survives not just this bizarre accident, but a hellish boarding school for Native American orphans, a well-meaning but wildly dysfunctional Mormon foster-family, and the loss of most of the illusions that are supposed to make life bearable. <br /><br />What persists is Edgar’s innate goodness, his belief in the redeeming power of language, and his determination to find and forgive the man who almost killed him.<strong> The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</strong> is a miracle of storytelling, bursting with heartache and hilarity and inhabited by characters as outsized as the landscape of the American West.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17612365159426462530">...more</a>

      </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_559987965">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559987965" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7099104-scott-axsom" class="left imgcol" title="Scott Axsom"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1413415144p2/7099104.jpg" alt="Scott Axsom" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/559987965?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7099104-scott-axsom" class="user" title="Scott Axsom">Scott Axsom</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer559987965">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18271082804734169824">Is there a word for that sweet spot a great author hits with the “voice” of his story - the one that lulls you into believing you’re leaving this world and entering his? Some writers hit it, off and on, throughout various works and, if they do it often enough, we ascribe to them the descriptors of greatness. But, what if the writer hits it from page one and never loses it? What do we call it then? Whatever the word, Brady Udall finds it, and keeps it, in <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</em>, perhaps t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18271082804734169824">Is there a word for that sweet spot a great author hits with the “voice” of his story - the one that lulls you into believing you’re leaving this world and entering his? Some writers hit it, off and on, throughout various works and, if they do it often enough, we ascribe to them the descriptors of greatness. But, what if the writer hits it from page one and never loses it? What do we call it then? Whatever the word, Brady Udall finds it, and keeps it, in <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</em>, perhaps the best debut novel I’ve read.<br /><br />Many have compared Udall to John Irving, another writer whose work I cherish, but Udall, I think, is the better of the two, at least on this outing. I loved <em>The World According to Garp</em> and <em>The Hotel New Hampshire</em> (among others), but neither brought me to the brink of literary ecstasy, as did <em>Edgar Mint</em>. By the end of this book – and this has <em>never</em> happened to me before – my hands were trembling as I turned the pages.<br /><br />The story is about a Native American (Apache) boy whose head is run over by a mail jeep, and the monumental changes the event brings to his future. The mere fact of his survival is considered by all to be a miracle (thus, the title, sort of), and I’m always inclined to look for biblical allegory in such cases. In the case of <em>Edgar Mint</em>, those inclinations are handsomely-rewarded.<br /><br />One would be naturally disposed to assume that Edgar’s survival of the accident was his resurrection but, as Udall makes abundantly clear, we’re not talking about Jesus here but of <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lazarus_of_Bethany">Lazarus</a>, and the events that constitute the rolling of the stone in front of Edgar’s tomb begin <em>after</em> he survives the accident. So, here’s where the John Irving comparisons undoubtedly arise; Udall takes the reader into a heart-breaking world of bullies and prejudice and, like Irving, he renders it as hilarious as it is distressing because Edgar often finds the outrageous injustice of it all comical himself. After all, he’s survived having his head run over by a jeep, after that, everything else is just child’s play. <br /><br />Though the book is written in the first person, the author occasionally utilizes the (maddening to some) tool of lapsing into the third person, when describing Edgar’s life. He does it rarely, and seemingly indiscriminately, and I began to wonder why. But here's the tell: Edgar wanders through his harsh world wondering if, perhaps, he’s actually watching someone <em>else’s</em> life, questioning if so much cruelty and misfortune could really be foisted upon one child. And, like anyone who’s ever suffered at the hands of caprice, he begins to wonder if he deserves it; if maybe it’s all his fault. Edgar’s losses brought me to despair again and again and, each time, Edgar would rise up and, together, we’d laugh at the inequity of it all until, finally, I wanted to shout, “Enough! Leave him alone!” And then came the resolution to Udall’s brilliant, if emotionally exhausting, work; then, he rolled the boulder away…<br /><br /><em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</em> left me with the realization that, despite life’s often capricious cruelty and injustice and ugliness, there is also hope and beauty and love and… resurrection. More importantly, Udall brings home the bare fact that, though fate is patently unfair, the inequity of it is not earned. It is simply one face of a many faceted jewel. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18271082804734169824">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_126067537">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/126067537" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2863914-j" class="left imgcol" title="j"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1407175214p2/2863914.jpg" alt="j" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/126067537?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 08, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2863914-j" class="user" title="j">j</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2863914-j?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2863914-j?shelf=52-in-2010" class="actionLinkLite">52-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer126067537">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1106609611076620438">If there is a fictional trope I could probably do without it is the precocious child narrator. I can only read so many books about weirdly bookish children with a story to tell, or a life of destiny to live. And yet it seems like every time you turn around, there is a new one being trumpeted for its "unique, pitch-perfect voice," <a title="Room by Emma Donoghue" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7478504.Room">sometimes by me</a>. Yeah, maybe I like some of these books (or <a title="Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close by Jonathan Safran Foer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4588.Extremely_Loud_and_Incredibly_Close">not especially</a>), and I'm even planning to read one soon that is <a title="The Instructions by Adam Levin" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8380409.The_Instructions">over 1,000 pages long</a> fergoshsakes. But in g</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1106609611076620438">If there is a fictional trope I could probably do without it is the precocious child narrator. I can only read so many books about weirdly bookish children with a story to tell, or a life of destiny to live. And yet it seems like every time you turn around, there is a new one being trumpeted for its "unique, pitch-perfect voice," <a title="Room by Emma Donoghue" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7478504.Room">sometimes by me</a>. Yeah, maybe I like some of these books (or <a title="Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close by Jonathan Safran Foer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4588.Extremely_Loud_and_Incredibly_Close">not especially</a>), and I'm even planning to read one soon that is <a title="The Instructions by Adam Levin" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8380409.The_Instructions">over 1,000 pages long</a> fergoshsakes. But in general, nine-year-olds are not super interesting as a rule, and it gets harder and harder to impress me by creating one that is "special" in a way that makes reading a book about her not <a title="The Sweetness at the Bottom of the Pie (Flavia de Luce, #1) by Alan Bradley" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6218281.The_Sweetness_at_the_Bottom_of_the_Pie__Flavia_de_Luce___1_">rather tiresome</a>.<br /><br />An easy way to get around this is the memoir novel. Sure, your lead is a precocious child. Sure, you want to use first-person voice. Just have it be narration from the character looking back over the years. It will require you to do less jumping through hoops to either write in an affected faux-simplistic style or make your narrator so extra-special that of course he uses big words and makes profound statements about the nature of existence (Jonathan Safran Foer is guilty of both of these annoying habits at the same time). So right off the bat, Brady Udall earns major points for doing just that: letting Edgar Mint tell us about his miracle childhood years after he's grown up and processed it, rather than trapping us in his limited worldview as it happens.<br /><br />Because I think that would be pretty frustrating: Edgar is a compelling character, but a frustrating one: born into a life of poverty on a Native American reservation and with a brain damaged by a terrible accident (head run over by a mail truck), he has plenty of affected quirks in place from the get-go: with no memory of growing up, he's naive about the world and his place in it; he can't write but loves to type (on an old fashioned typewriter, natch); he constantly wets the bed; because he survived what should have been a certain death, he considers himself touched by God and thus spends a lot of time contemplating religion; &amp;c. It's pretty clear this "miracle life" is going to involve a lot of slightly fantastical adventures (and it does: death-defying scrapes with bullies at a school for orphans, daring escapes in the night, a quest to find his missing mother, living with Mormons). All of this plus a child narrator would be too much to take.<br /><br />But the adult Edgar proves a very amenable tour guide to this miracle life, allowing the child's POV to breathe while inserting just the right degree of perspective. Udall is also very elegant with his prose; both of his novels are consistently funny and thoughtful in equal measure. That other book is <a title="The Lonely Polygamist by Brady Udall" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6944566.The_Lonely_Polygamist">The Lonely Polygamist</a>, which is still my favorite book of 2010 (and which I <em>still</em> haven't reviewed), and that one's a lot better, but The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint is definitely a good start. It drags a bit too much in the middle, which is why I'm knocking it down a star, but the destination is definitely worth the journey to get there: I can think of few books that end quite as satisfyingly. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1106609611076620438">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_675191568">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/675191568" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="left imgcol" title="Larry Bassett"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1390874855p2/3816725.jpg" alt="Larry Bassett" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/675191568?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett" class="user" title="Larry Bassett">Larry Bassett</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer675191568'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer675191568">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10557675628160232991">When I was visiting my ninety-two year old father in Michigan, he was reading a book and occasionally chortling to himself. When he finished the book, he told me I should read it and could borrow his copy. So what could I do? I brought <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</em> home to read!<br /><br />The book starts out with an exclamation mark!<br /><blockquote> If I could tell you only one thing about my life it would be that when I was seven years old the mailman ran over my head. As formative events go, nothing else comes close; m</blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10557675628160232991">When I was visiting my ninety-two year old father in Michigan, he was reading a book and occasionally chortling to himself. When he finished the book, he told me I should read it and could borrow his copy. So what could I do? I brought <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</em> home to read!<br /><br />The book starts out with an exclamation mark!<br /><blockquote> If I could tell you only one thing about my life it would be that when I was seven years old the mailman ran over my head. As formative events go, nothing else comes close; my careening, zigzag existence, my wounded brain and faith in God, my collision with joy and affliction, all of it has come, in one way or another, out of that moment on a summer morning when the left rear tire of a United States postal jeep ground my tiny head into the hot gravel of the San Carlos Apache Indian Reservation. </blockquote><br />This accident is the precursor to the Miracle alluded to in the title of the book. <br /><blockquote> First, I had survived a mail jeep running over my head. Then, after three months in a coma, I had simply awakened, almost without warning and with only minimal brain damage. According to science and simple common sense, I should have been a vegetable, lucky to spend the rest of my days diapered and spoon-fed, my skull full of jelly. But I was progressing so well the doctors didn’t know what to make of me; they’d shake their heads, muttering under their breaths, checking and rechecking their charts, utterly perplexed, as if my continuing miracle was causing them to lose faith in the things they’d held most sacred all along. </blockquote><br />Edgar had survived and astounded the medical profession at St. Devine’s Hospital. It was time for him to move on to the next venue: the Willie Sherman School for Native American orphans in Arizona is his new home. Edgar’s mother was an Indian. As an orphan, Edgar is obligated to cycle through several less than satisfactory living arrangements. The book is the story of his life.<br /><br />About halfway through the book, an important thing happens: two missionaries carrying <a title="The Book of Mormon Another Testament of Jesus Christ by The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/323355.The_Book_of_Mormon_Another_Testament_of_Jesus_Christ"> The Book of Mormon </a> come into the story. These missionaries ride bikes, are from out of town, wear nametags, and are named Elder Spafford and Elder Turley. They are probably among the 48,757 people who have given <em>The Book of Mormon</em> an average 4.46 rating on GR. This is a very high rating. In fact, I have never seen any rating quite so high.<br /><br />If you have ever had an experience of neatly dressed strangers coming to your door to save you, you will recognize this segment of the book. It is funny. Maybe not so funny if you take religion very seriously. No worries for me on that count! Edgar, who was seven when the jeep ran over his head, is now twelve and is ready to be baptized so he can be placed in a Mormon foster home. Next stop: Richland, Utah.<br /><br />Edgar is attached to a Hermes Jubilee typewriter and is constantly typing out his thoughts. He says he has 11,789 pages packed in his trunk when he arrives in Richland. The story of him settling into his new foster home is nicely done, capturing the experience sensitively. He has some big changes to accommodate into his young life: puberty, religion, pseudo siblings, a troubled foster family. At a young age, Edgar has already had a number of encounters with death – including very nearly his own – that leave him with considerable anger and a desire to kill God with his pocket knife.<br /><br />One of his goals is to find the mailman who ran over his head and let him know that he survived. The trail to this man takes him away from his Richland home. He is now fifteen and destined to meet his past in Pennsylvania and to learn more about the man devastated by Edgar’s “death” at the age of seven. Edgar locates his widow, Rosa, and realizes that she and her deceased husband had nearly been the parents who could have changed the entire trajectory of his life. <br /><blockquote> But there has been no greater blessing than Rosa. For thirteen years she and I did one simple thing: we were good to each other. We got each other drinks, We said <em>please</em> and <em>thank you</em> and <em>doesn’t that shirt look nice.</em> We bought cards for each other on Valentine’s Day and found inordinate pleasure in watching reruns of “The Benny Hill Show.” We took turns cleaning the toilet. We talked bad about the neighbors and made fun of the persnickety old widows who liked to stand up front during liturgy and show off their new permanents. We played Scrabble and Yahtzee and let each other get away with murder. </blockquote><br />The book ends on a saccharinely sweet note that leaves a lump in your throat. Rosa dies peacefully and Edgar lives happily ever after – a miraculous life with lots of bumps – not all of them on his once crushed head. <br /><br />I quoted the first paragraph at the beginning of this review so let me make the perfect bookends by quoting the last paragraph at the end:<br /><blockquote> I’m supposed to meet Mitzi at Klutsner’s Deli for lunch to celebrate this new stage in our relationship, and I have a little time, so I roll a clean sheet into my typewriter and let me fingers have their way. In awhile, after I have added a few more inconsequential words and pages to this sprawling pile, I will put on my coat, pick up my Hermes Jubilee, lock the doors behind me, and emerge from the shadows of this house into the bright day, blinking and holding my hand to the sky, amazed at the light, like a man raised from the dead. </blockquote><br />I never would have read this sweet and corny book without the encouragement (OK, requirement) of my Dad. I will enjoy telling him that I gave <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint</em> four stars. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10557675628160232991">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2188118-claudia-kieselhorst?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer179039927">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8360382074236161459">This is one of those few books that should not have any details disclosed about it - no spoiler alerts, no hints, no outlines or summaries. I would even vote to remove the descriptions from the back covers of every copy in existence. Here's why...<br /><br />The beauty of this story comes from the reader's innocence and the display of curiosity and interest that occurs with every turn of the page. If I divulged even the tiniest detail, it would surely remove one of the magical sparks of this book, and for t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8360382074236161459">This is one of those few books that should not have any details disclosed about it - no spoiler alerts, no hints, no outlines or summaries. I would even vote to remove the descriptions from the back covers of every copy in existence. Here's why...<br /><br />The beauty of this story comes from the reader's innocence and the display of curiosity and interest that occurs with every turn of the page. If I divulged even the tiniest detail, it would surely remove one of the magical sparks of this book, and for that I applaud Udall. He has woven together (not told) a story that is, at once, spell-binding, elusive, heartwarming, tear-jerking, unbelievable, and yet totally believable. And, if you get as emotionally entangled with the characters and their stories as I did, it might even leave you a bit angry - attempting a bargain with the author and asking, "But why couldn't their life be a bit easier?" <br /><br />Please stop whatever book you are reading and pick up a copy of this wonderful tale. Your heart will absolutely swoon at the delicate and masterful placement of words and sentences, seemingly simple in first appearance, but that dig deep into your mind so that you will be recalling them (and their substantial meaning) months later. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8360382074236161459">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17090316790243630230">I guess the best way to put this is to say that the things I liked about this novel make up for the things I didn't.<br />Interesting protagist. Excellent pace. Funny when it should be and kind of depressing when it shouldn't. For the most part, Udall should be given credit for making so much that doesn't seem like it could ever happen seem like a natural progression in the life of his characters. <br /><br />By the end, however, I was feeling a bit of the old "Oh, come on!" by the novel's turn of events. I was a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17090316790243630230">I guess the best way to put this is to say that the things I liked about this novel make up for the things I didn't.<br />Interesting protagist. Excellent pace. Funny when it should be and kind of depressing when it shouldn't. For the most part, Udall should be given credit for making so much that doesn't seem like it could ever happen seem like a natural progression in the life of his characters. <br /><br />By the end, however, I was feeling a bit of the old "Oh, come on!" by the novel's turn of events. I was also annoyed by the constant shift from first- to third-person. This shift in tense was often in the middle of a paragraph or sentence (example: "I really enjoyed this book and think it's the best book Aaron has read all year!") and I'm not really certain what purpose it served.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_53398292">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53398292" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1157650-ender" class="left imgcol" title="Ender"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1350247114p2/1157650.jpg" alt="Ender" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/53398292?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 15, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1157650-ender" class="user" title="Ender">Ender</a>

        rated it

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer53398292">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11736326685559485459">I love to be so pleasantly surprised by a book. Reading something like this is difficult, because I struggle greatly with obvious injustice, but there was the constant comfort of Edgar's attitude telling me that it was all okay. <br /><br />I love the way there was so little happening, and yet so much happening at the same time. I loved the way Edgar seemed to be oblivious to so many little nuances of what was happening, yet, even though he was the story teller, the reader knew EXACTLY what was going on. <br /><br />It</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11736326685559485459">I love to be so pleasantly surprised by a book. Reading something like this is difficult, because I struggle greatly with obvious injustice, but there was the constant comfort of Edgar's attitude telling me that it was all okay. <br /><br />I love the way there was so little happening, and yet so much happening at the same time. I loved the way Edgar seemed to be oblivious to so many little nuances of what was happening, yet, even though he was the story teller, the reader knew EXACTLY what was going on. <br /><br />It was like watching your own child learning something for the first time, in some cases with great joy, and in others with deep sorrow.<br /><br />He was so misunderstood by casual observers, but so completely understood by those who actually took the time to care. So complex and yet so simple, like all of us.<br /><br /><br />***spoiler-ish references to the ending***<br /><br /><br /><br />And I love the ending. It's still a tragedy, but somehow a tragedy that made me feel really good. <br /><br />Having the Mormon part was a funny surprise to me, but I thought the author was very gracious and believable. While he certainly wasn't pro-anything, he was completely genuine and I thought it was wonderful. </span>

  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/1157650-ender" alt="Ender"><img alt="1157650" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1350247114p1/1157650.jpg" /></a>
  </div>
  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a href="/user/show/1157650-ender" style="font-weight:bold">Ender</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer2819899223848360909"><em>Carla wrote: "You must have a greater capacity for human degradation than I do. After suffering through 400 pages of it, I felt so assaulted and degra</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2819899223848360909"><em>Carla wrote: "You must have a greater capacity for human degradation than I do. After suffering through 400 pages of it, I felt so assaulted and degraded that 20 pages of semi sweet redemptive wrap-up could comf..."</em><br /><br />I can certainly understand your reaction. There are indeed many evils in the world and for our own peace of mind sometimes we have to plug our ears and pretend it isn't so, because there isn't always anything we can do to help, so we only feel pain and sorrow. <br /><br />I think it's a good experience for us to know the pain in the world. Our complacency might just keep us from rescuing the Edgars out there that we want to pretend don't exist.<br /><br />I'm sure your friends who recommended the book thought the story was authentic and important and didn't consider how painful it would be for you to take that journey. Still, I hope you are a stronger person because of it. When I visit Cambodia or part of Mexico I find that even Edgar didn't have it as bad as real children all over the world do. Yes, that's even more pain, but I don't think we should ignore the sad parts of the human condition just so we can remain comfortably oblivious.<br /><br />Thank you for your kind words. I hope your next literary journey is more rewarding.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_461913330">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/461913330" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/602881-paul" class="left imgcol" title="Paul"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1396490548p2/602881.jpg" alt="Paul" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/461913330?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/602881-paul" class="user" title="Paul">Paul</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer461913330">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1591268619170762281">Having read Udall’s The Lonely Polygamist earlier this year and having been blown away by it (I gave that one also 5 stars out of 5), I knew I simply had to get ahold of the rest of his books and see if The Lonely Polygamist was a fluke of genius or if Udall really IS that good. Well, by the time I was only a few pages into this book, I was convinced that Udall really IS that good. So good, in fact, that he has quickly become one of my favorite authors.<br /><br />As with Polygamist, this book was one where</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1591268619170762281">Having read Udall’s The Lonely Polygamist earlier this year and having been blown away by it (I gave that one also 5 stars out of 5), I knew I simply had to get ahold of the rest of his books and see if The Lonely Polygamist was a fluke of genius or if Udall really IS that good. Well, by the time I was only a few pages into this book, I was convinced that Udall really IS that good. So good, in fact, that he has quickly become one of my favorite authors.<br /><br />As with Polygamist, this book was one where I hung on every word, deliberately taking my time in reading it so that I could stop and enjoy the writing throughout. Sure, the plot was exciting and captivating enough to make me want to read it much more quickly, but I just couldn’t help but stopping to smell the literary roses along the way. <br /><br />As an example, one of the first scenes—that of Edgar Mint getting run over by a mail truck—would be one which should evoke nothing but sadness and horror in any reader. Udall, though, found a way to actually make me laugh out loud at some of it, and even stop to wonder how I could laugh at something so morbid! He is just that good, that powerful a writer. <br /><br />This book was really incredibly captivating, raw, gut-wrenching, and heartstring-tugging. The surprising ending was also something which was just perfect—something that can’t be said about many other works of genius that sometimes fall flat at their ends but stand on the merit of their middle pages. Udall’s writing is just wonderful from beginning to end, and I recommend any and all of his books to anyone who wants to get REALLY lost in a REAL book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1591268619170762281">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14471462975800378460">Certainly the best book I've read in some time. A year or so ago a woman put the book in my hands and said, read this; it's amazing. I filed it away, so innondated as I was with the pile of books-to-be-read. Then a few weeks ago I attended a booksellers conferance and a speaker on handselling referenced it, saying he loved the book so much that he and his bookstore sold 400 in hardcover and 1,100 in paperback. That is a lot of love. <br /> So, I sat down with Edgar Mint one afternoon and didn't move f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14471462975800378460">Certainly the best book I've read in some time. A year or so ago a woman put the book in my hands and said, read this; it's amazing. I filed it away, so innondated as I was with the pile of books-to-be-read. Then a few weeks ago I attended a booksellers conferance and a speaker on handselling referenced it, saying he loved the book so much that he and his bookstore sold 400 in hardcover and 1,100 in paperback. That is a lot of love. <br /> So, I sat down with Edgar Mint one afternoon and didn't move from that spot for three days. Ok, so that's a lie, but the sentiment is true. I have fallen in love with the brave, naive Apache kid who can't keep his head out of trouble. I followed behind him as he made each step in his tumultuous life. And at the end of the book I lived his joy with him. Isn't that why we read?<br /></span>

  <a href="/user/show/2243098-mmars" class="left imgcol" title="Mmars"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1337885673p2/2243098.jpg" alt="Mmars" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/503242163?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 22, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2243098-mmars" class="user" title="Mmars">Mmars</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer503242163">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1661314319473910713">4.5/5<br /><br />Brady Udall's character Edgar Mint is a survivor. As in "one who survives." By all means he should have been dead, would sometimes rather have been dead, and rarely took action to avert situations in which he could have died. At seven years of age he wakes from a coma unable to remember his life to that point. A mail truck had run over his head and he had been rescued by Barry, a young, unethical and rogue doctor whose sole purpose in life is to save Edgar from that fateful day forward. But</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1661314319473910713">4.5/5<br /><br />Brady Udall's character Edgar Mint is a survivor. As in "one who survives." By all means he should have been dead, would sometimes rather have been dead, and rarely took action to avert situations in which he could have died. At seven years of age he wakes from a coma unable to remember his life to that point. A mail truck had run over his head and he had been rescued by Barry, a young, unethical and rogue doctor whose sole purpose in life is to save Edgar from that fateful day forward. But rather, Barry only leaves ruin behind as he loses his job and becomes a user and small-time drug dealer.<br /><br />But the focus here is Edgar, the narrator who sometimes refers to himself in the first person and sometimes the third, hinting at an understandable detachment from his miserable life. First, he is a half-breed child with and alcoholic mother. After the accident, he receives negligible treatment in a reservation hospital. He is released to an uncle whose job gets Edgar into a boarding school peopled with delinquents, orphans, and misfits and rampant with bullying and steered by an inept principal. And then comes Utah and a non-practicing Mormon family who take in any human or animal in need of mending. Everything should have gotten better at this point. But no, enter doctor Barry.<br /><br />This may all sound quite bleak, but other than an excessive amout of descriptive and tragic bullying episodes, it is often humorous. Edgar is an endearing character who unintentionally worms his way into the hearts of both the reader and the more humane adults he encounters.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1661314319473910713">...more</a>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10697484057265921524">Wow! I thoroughly enjoyed this book. I must admit I was leery for a while, for my mind had no clue as to what little boys and young men think about. It was hard for me to get past the "initiation" type activities of school mates. This book was so intriguing, the author knows how to grab your attention, and equally important, how to hold it. As I read I always have questions and he answered them, all of them. I finally get to add something to my favorites shelf. I will definitely be looking for more books by Brady Udall. It seems he teaches at a University in Southern Illinois. (At least he did at the time this book was published.) Lucky students...............he is a master.<br />On the back cover it says,"If I could tell you only one thing about my life it would be this: when I was seven years old the mailman ran over my head. As formative events go, nothing else come close." I laughed out loud and I cried. I hope you all, male and female, enjoy this book as much as I did. Thank you Mr. Udall!!!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12886602009980622753">It is going to be tough to write a review to do this book justice. Here is what I can tell you about it. The writing is rich. The characters are so unqiue and real, it is a wonder to get to know them all. The story is brilliantly woven through time and history to maximize the impact on the reader. The book explores the biggest themes and questions we face - how we make sense of tragedy, how we understand loss, what it means to be human, how religion can become a part of our existence, and how we</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12886602009980622753">It is going to be tough to write a review to do this book justice. Here is what I can tell you about it. The writing is rich. The characters are so unqiue and real, it is a wonder to get to know them all. The story is brilliantly woven through time and history to maximize the impact on the reader. The book explores the biggest themes and questions we face - how we make sense of tragedy, how we understand loss, what it means to be human, how religion can become a part of our existence, and how we find meaning in our past and ultimately in our lives. I loved this book from the first chapter, and I enjoyed the journey the entire way. It joins the ranks as one of my top 5 books. It was profound - it touched me deeply and left me feeling incredible joy, sorrow, pain, and satisfaction. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/104764873?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 29, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1508396-cindi" class="user" title="Cindi">Cindi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1508396-cindi?shelf=2010" class="actionLinkLite">2010</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer104764873">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13458043213336348525">After reading a review of Udall's newest novel "The Loneliest Polygamist", I was intrigued, did a little research regarding the author and discovered his acclaimed novel "The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint".<br /><br />Edgar Mint is an authentic and sympathetic character. He is lovable. I found myself rooting for him during all the unfortunate events of his young life. Following an accident in which seven-year-old Edgar's head is crushed by a mail jeep, Edgar is brought back to life and the "miracles" continue.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13458043213336348525">After reading a review of Udall's newest novel "The Loneliest Polygamist", I was intrigued, did a little research regarding the author and discovered his acclaimed novel "The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint".<br /><br />Edgar Mint is an authentic and sympathetic character. He is lovable. I found myself rooting for him during all the unfortunate events of his young life. Following an accident in which seven-year-old Edgar's head is crushed by a mail jeep, Edgar is brought back to life and the "miracles" continue.<br /><br />This book is peopled with unique, strange and curious characters. Dr. Barry Pinkley is definitely one of the oddest characters. He reminds me of "Lolita's" Humbert, although the motivations behind Barry's obsession with Edgar are apparently different.<br /><br />Udall, a fabulous writer, creates an essentially depressing story and yet never lets the reader get depressed. There is always hope. Edgar is always hopeful. <br /><br />The writing is witty, irreverent, and even funny. It is also crass and vulgar and describes some of the more dark realities of our world. I would advise my more sensitive friends in the book club to avoid it. Everyone else should find it, read it and enjoy.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13458043213336348525">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/613566688?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 24, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/962663-karen" class="user" title="Karen">Karen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/962663-karen?shelf=us-multicultural" class="actionLinkLite">us-multicultural</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/962663-karen?shelf=latter-day-saint" class="actionLinkLite">latter-day-saint</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/962663-karen?shelf=western-regional" class="actionLinkLite">western-regional</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/962663-karen?shelf=coming-of-age" class="actionLinkLite">coming-of-age</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer613566688">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3586266949962099576">Udall chronicles the life of one odd little boy, Edgar Mint. He is subjective to a number of hardships starting with an absent father and an alcoholic mother, but things go downhill fast for him from there. Most painful are the chapters about his years at a boarding school for troubled Native American kids. He is brutally bullied and either ostracized or coerced into criminal behavior by a couple of hoodlums who are older, bigger and more brutal than he. Year after year people close to him disap</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3586266949962099576">Udall chronicles the life of one odd little boy, Edgar Mint. He is subjective to a number of hardships starting with an absent father and an alcoholic mother, but things go downhill fast for him from there. Most painful are the chapters about his years at a boarding school for troubled Native American kids. He is brutally bullied and either ostracized or coerced into criminal behavior by a couple of hoodlums who are older, bigger and more brutal than he. Year after year people close to him disappear or betray him, but someone he soldiers on. <br /><br />The book toys with ideas of fate, divine purpose, belonging, loyalty, betrayal and the quest for home. It's oddly sweet and optimistic--even when the characters are swimming around in the sewage of inhumanity. Somehow Udall manages to convey what makes even the mean and the dysfunctional tick. Everyone has a story, and Udall is adept at weaving these stories together to tell Edgar's story as well as those of his wide-ranging supportive cast. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3586266949962099576">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_102324792">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102324792" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3715708-kate" class="left imgcol" title="Kate"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351598363p2/3715708.jpg" alt="Kate" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/102324792?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 11, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3715708-kate" class="user" title="Kate">Kate</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer102324792">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10562624345745330076">Edgar Mint's childhood mission is locating the mailman who ran over his head as a child. He wants to assure him that he's OK. <br />This mission is the focus of Brady Udall's The Miracle of Edgar Mint. The author's inspiration to write Edgar's story developed from something much less painful than having his head run over, although possibly equally as dramatic. <br /><br />The peculiar story that gave Udall the framework to work was sparked when his then girlfriend, now wife, revealed she was dating another man. S</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10562624345745330076">Edgar Mint's childhood mission is locating the mailman who ran over his head as a child. He wants to assure him that he's OK. <br />This mission is the focus of Brady Udall's The Miracle of Edgar Mint. The author's inspiration to write Edgar's story developed from something much less painful than having his head run over, although possibly equally as dramatic. <br /><br />The peculiar story that gave Udall the framework to work was sparked when his then girlfriend, now wife, revealed she was dating another man. She told him about the other man and how as a child he'd been run over by a mailman. Udall actively sought this man out. <br /><br />"He thought I was going to beat him up," Udall says. <br /><br />But he was too interested in his history to hurt him. The other man's story was the inspiration for Edgar's. <br /><br />The Miracle of Edgar Mint is Udall's first novel, although he has written many short stories. <br /><br />"The hard part is getting the confidence and figuring it out." Udall says. "It's a huge investment of time and energy. This book took three and a half years." <br /><br />That time was spent shaping and perfecting the story of a young boy facing hardships that most would find daunting, yet he perseveres. Run over at 7, Edgar spends months in a hospital until he's sent to live with an uncle he's never met on an Indian reservation and goes to a school where he's tormented daily by classmates. Edgar manages to flee the school to the home of a Mormon family willing to take him in, but his problems continue. <br /><br />Udall wrote the story in first and third person in part because Edgar's story is so unusual and also because it's a grown Edgar telling us the story of his childhood. <br /><br />"Edgar is a character in his own story," Udall says. "He's aware he's a character in his own story." <br /><br />This makes the jumps between reading Edgar's story from his own point of view or from an outside narrator as logical as the jumps between the settings of Edgar's life. <br /><br />Although some of the locations in The Miracle of Edgar Mint were fictional, others, such as Edgar's school, were real places near where Udall grew up in Arizona. With so many settings for Edgar's experiences, Udall could have done extensive research to get each detail right. Although this is what he started to do, eventually he stopped researching at all. <br /><br />"Imagination with a few good details is enough, with the right attitude," Udall says. "I like to have the freedom to make it the way I want." <br /><br />In The Miracle of Edgar Mint, Udall let his imagination run rampant. <br /><br />"I grew up in a big stable Mormon family, the polar opposite of what Edgar grew up with," he says. <br /><br />Edgar experiences more turmoil in 10 years than most people do in a lifetime. Still, Udall manages to show the humorous side of otherwise depressing circumstances. <br /><br />An admirer of the writing of Mark Twain, Udall sees writing comedy as an ultimate achievement. <br /><br />"It's not easy to take depressing, bleak writing and inject it with humor," he says. <br /><br />Not easy, perhaps, but that's exactly what he does. For instance, while recovering at the hospital, Edgar is surrounded by people in hopeless situations. And while sad, when he turns to a urinal puck for comfort, there's something darkly comedic about his deodorizing security blanket. <br /><br />Lightening the mood in what could be a dark story is also Edgar's unabashed motivation to live. As the character himself says in reference to himself and the people he befriends during his hospital stay, "We were broken and afflicted and maybe ... we could make ourselves whole again." <br /><br />Edgar's mission to locate the mailman from his past seems to be the key to making himself whole. Udall tried to reflect in Edgar the motivation he saw in kids from troubled backgrounds. <br /><br />"I tried to place myself as a child." Udall says. "I've talked to lots of children. They're not aware that there's an alternative. They don't believe they have a choice." <br /><br />Unaware, perhaps, that he can do anything but carry on, Edgar keeps going, trying to make himself whole. In so doing, his story becomes an endearing one of survival. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10562624345745330076">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_95174516">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95174516" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/914291-denice" class="left imgcol" title="Denice"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1379018568p2/914291.jpg" alt="Denice" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/95174516?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 22, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/914291-denice" class="user" title="Denice">Denice</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer95174516">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17952955925557279686">I picked this up as an audiobook from Audible.com because Brady Udall is going to be one of the featured speakers at The Festival of Faith and Writing for which I had registered. I'd not heard of the book nor the author, so I wasn't sure what to expect.<br /><br />Throughout the book, I wavered back and forth between, "Wow. I can't wait to hear what happens next," and "Good Lord, I can't listen to another minute of this." Being one who is not a big fan of profanity or flippant references to sex in a book, I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17952955925557279686">I picked this up as an audiobook from Audible.com because Brady Udall is going to be one of the featured speakers at The Festival of Faith and Writing for which I had registered. I'd not heard of the book nor the author, so I wasn't sure what to expect.<br /><br />Throughout the book, I wavered back and forth between, "Wow. I can't wait to hear what happens next," and "Good Lord, I can't listen to another minute of this." Being one who is not a big fan of profanity or flippant references to sex in a book, I shared with a few people that the book was intriguing, but the language and some of the content was unseemly, that I wasn't sure I would even finish it, that I certainly couldn't recommend it. <br /><br />But now that I've finished the book, Edgar Mint has won a place in my heart, and I have to say, in spite of the language and the really shocking and terrible things that happen to Edgar, and in spite of the number of times I yelled at the character to "DO something about the issue, doggoneit! TELL someone!", I have come to the conclusion that, yes, Brady Udall is an amazing writer, that the characters he created in this book came alive to me, that I was surprised by the turn of events and pleased with the end result. <br /><br />Can I recommend this book now? It depends on who I'm talking to. Edgar Mint is not for the reader of light-hearted fiction. It's tough stuff, gritty and infuriating and ugly and sad. But it's also very funny and real and honest and satisfying. <br /><br />And now that I've discovered a conflict with the schedule and I'm not able to attend the Festival of Faith and Writing, I'm totally bummed that I won't be able to sit in on a session with Brady Udall. I'd love to get into his head, beyond what he opened up for us with The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17952955925557279686">...more</a>

          </span>

      <a href="/user/show/1316491-savanna" class="user" title="Savanna">Savanna</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

      An English professor at the college I was about to attend

            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1316491-savanna?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer26888331">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11089533544476532072">This is an absolutely delightful, enthralling novel. I read it during my last month in high school--in hardcover. It all starts when the protagonist gets run over by a mail truck on his reservation as a small child. More specifically, his head gets run over. That sounds like an awful place to start, but it's perfect. <br /><br />I have distinct memories of reading this book during calculus, after we'd taken the AP. In those last weeks of the year, we were still required to go to calculus class even though t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11089533544476532072">This is an absolutely delightful, enthralling novel. I read it during my last month in high school--in hardcover. It all starts when the protagonist gets run over by a mail truck on his reservation as a small child. More specifically, his head gets run over. That sounds like an awful place to start, but it's perfect. <br /><br />I have distinct memories of reading this book during calculus, after we'd taken the AP. In those last weeks of the year, we were still required to go to calculus class even though there was nothing new to learn and no assignments to fulfill. All of the students in the class were given the opportunity to improve their final grade by doing extra worksheets. My grade could definitely have used improvement, but I was the only girl in the class who passed up the worksheets. I opted for Edgar Mint, and I don't regret it a bit.<br /><br />This is a book I recommend to friends who don't tend to read or enjoy novels. I made the mistake of lending my copy to a boyfriend of that description several years ago. We broke up a few months later and I never got Edgar back. You might be surprised by how frequently I consider contacting him just to ask for the book back. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11089533544476532072">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2767068228825926481">Sometimes I lament the limitations of the Goodreads rating system. Three stars means I liked it? Well, I didn't like it. I mostly hated it. Many sections made me cringe. I frequently had to close the book and try to think fluffy-rainbow-unicorn thoughts. And yet it was compelling--well thought-out and well-written.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText2767068228825926481">Sometimes I lament the limitations of the Goodreads rating system. Three stars means I liked it? Well, I didn't like it. I mostly hated it. Many sections made me cringe. I frequently had to close the book and try to think fluffy-rainbow-unicorn thoughts. And yet it was compelling--well thought-out and well-written. I did struggle with the frequent change in point-of-view. I want to trust that the author had good reason for employing such a technique, some sort of mirror to Edgar's psyche and the way that he copes with the trauma in his past. However, it felt gimmicky. But again, it was such a compelling exploration of the human spirit, and I feel that Udall's writing was courageous and ultimately successful. </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/2209427-holly" class="user" title="Holly">Holly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1267028.The_Miracle_Life_Of_Edgar_Mint" title="Hardcover 9780393020366" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2209427-holly?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2209427-holly?shelf=mormon-stuff" class="actionLinkLite">mormon-stuff</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2209427-holly?shelf=pompous-dreck" class="actionLinkLite">pompous-dreck</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1059771807">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8723937265778067300">oh lordy.<br /><br />I bought a copy of this when it came out because it was by someone I knew (and liked) and about the part of the world where I'm from. I grew up about an hour from the San Carlos Apache Indian Reservation (and even taught high school there as an adult). <br /><br />And I really disliked this book. My main reaction was pretty much, "Oh, come on!" Seriously? It's not enough for the protagonist just to be an unfortunate kid from the rez--there are plenty of those--no, he has to have had his skull crush</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8723937265778067300">oh lordy.<br /><br />I bought a copy of this when it came out because it was by someone I knew (and liked) and about the part of the world where I'm from. I grew up about an hour from the San Carlos Apache Indian Reservation (and even taught high school there as an adult). <br /><br />And I really disliked this book. My main reaction was pretty much, "Oh, come on!" Seriously? It's not enough for the protagonist just to be an unfortunate kid from the rez--there are plenty of those--no, he has to have had his skull crushed by a mail truck? And have been saved by a crazy doctor who can't keep his medical license? And he has to switch back and forth between first and third person to tell his story?<br /><br />I should have heeded the comparisons of Udall to John Irving. I've read several of Irving's books, and I'm not a fan: he's gratuitously cruel to his characters, derivative (<em>A Prayer for Owen Meany,</em> for instance, is a bad ripoff of <em>The Scarlet Letter</em>, with a little bit of <em>Waterland</em> by Graham Swift thrown in) and pretentious.<br /><br />Ultimately the main thing I remember from the book is the scene where Rotten Teeth forces Edgar to swallow a turd. <em>Yeah,</em> I think. <em>I know the feeling.</em> <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8723937265778067300">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/1962591-aine-o-mahoney" class="left imgcol" title="Aine O'Mahoney"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1237213202p2/1962591.jpg" alt="Aine O'Mahoney" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/47277092?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 06, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1962591-aine-o-mahoney" class="user" title="Aine O'Mahoney">Aine O'Mahoney</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/167313.The_Miracle_Life_Of_Edgar_Mint" title="Paperback 9780099286769" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer47277092">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16314361856269247723">Edgar Mint<br /><br /><br />One thing that saves the Miracle life of Edgar Mint from being an uphill struggle is the boy himself, Edgar. He is an exceptional individual who has more to deal with than any kid ever should, but he rolls with the punches and get on with life in his own inimatible way. <br /><br />Some of the characters and situations Edgar encounters are of a very sinister nature but Udall never portrays them as such, even the darkest moments in Edgars life are smacked with humour. You find yourself smiling at t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16314361856269247723">Edgar Mint<br /><br /><br />One thing that saves the Miracle life of Edgar Mint from being an uphill struggle is the boy himself, Edgar. He is an exceptional individual who has more to deal with than any kid ever should, but he rolls with the punches and get on with life in his own inimatible way. <br /><br />Some of the characters and situations Edgar encounters are of a very sinister nature but Udall never portrays them as such, even the darkest moments in Edgars life are smacked with humour. You find yourself smiling at the most unlikely scenarios, punctuated with style and wit that smacks of John Irving.<br /><br />In the second chapter of his life Edgar spends time in a school for delinquents, it was this part of the book where I felt as though I was serving time with him, such was the monotony. But maybe this was a purposeful lack of editing on the authors behalf to enable the reader to experience the mundanity of life for Edgar at this time. <br /><br />That said, I fell in love with Edgar an amazing little boy that life just happened to, he takes knocks with class, individuality and style.. There is something so humble and human about Edgar we could all learn something from him.. If the first half of the book was an uphill struggle, the second half was free wheeling all the way..... Unputdownable! !<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16314361856269247723">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a href="/review/show/102662597?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 06, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2907291-glenda" class="user" title="Glenda">Glenda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2907291-glenda?shelf=books-read-in-2011" class="actionLinkLite">books-read-in-2011</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer102662597">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10014206029608252681">Half Apache and mostly orphaned, Edgar's trials begin on an Arizona reservation at the age of seven, when the mailman's jeep accidentally runs over his head. From the hospital to a school for delinquents to a Mormon foster family, comedy, pain, and trouble accompany Edgar through a string of larger-than-life experiences. Through it all, readers will root for this irresistible innocent who never truly loses heart, and whose quest for the mailman leads him to an unexpected home.<br />I fell in love with</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10014206029608252681">Half Apache and mostly orphaned, Edgar's trials begin on an Arizona reservation at the age of seven, when the mailman's jeep accidentally runs over his head. From the hospital to a school for delinquents to a Mormon foster family, comedy, pain, and trouble accompany Edgar through a string of larger-than-life experiences. Through it all, readers will root for this irresistible innocent who never truly loses heart, and whose quest for the mailman leads him to an unexpected home.<br />I fell in love with Edgar, an amazing little boy that life just happened to. There is something so humble and human about Edgar, we could all learn something from him. Edgar Mint has won a place in my heart, and I have to say, in spite of the language and the really shocking and terrible things that happen to Edgar, I have to give this book 5 stars, although I won't be recommending it to everyone I know.<br />It's tough stuff, gritty and infuriating and ugly and sad. But it's also very funny and real and honest and satisfying.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10014206029608252681">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_475783355">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475783355" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4982440-5inabus" class="left imgcol" title="5inabus"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="5inabus" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/475783355?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 02, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4982440-5inabus" class="user" title="5inabus">5inabus</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4982440-5inabus?shelf=best-all-time-ever" class="actionLinkLite">best-all-time-ever</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4982440-5inabus?shelf=i-own-it" class="actionLinkLite">i-own-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer475783355">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10635929640226233901">The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint is the Perfect Novel. Here is a list of the essential components of the Perfect Novel :<br /><br /><strong>1) The Perfect Novel is almost impossible to review well.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em> <br />This review, clearly. For better summaries I recommend <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559987965">Scott's</a> equally glowing but much more coherent review. For a quick synopsis, the back blurb has put into words what I cannot: <em>"Half-Apache and mostly orphaned, the adventures of Edgar Presely Mint begin on an Arizona reservation at the age of seven when th</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10635929640226233901">The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint is the Perfect Novel. Here is a list of the essential components of the Perfect Novel :<br /><br /><strong>1) The Perfect Novel is almost impossible to review well.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em> <br />This review, clearly. For better summaries I recommend <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559987965">Scott's</a> equally glowing but much more coherent review. For a quick synopsis, the back blurb has put into words what I cannot: <em>"Half-Apache and mostly orphaned, the adventures of Edgar Presely Mint begin on an Arizona reservation at the age of seven when the mailman's jeep accidently runs over his head. Shunted from the hospital to a reform school to a Mormon foster family, comedy and trouble accompany Edgar - the irresistible innocent who never truly loses heart, and whose quest for the mailman eventually leads him to an unexpected home." </em><br /><br /><strong>2) The Perfect Novel has characters so real that you become convinced that THEY live in the real world and you live in a made-up one.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em><br />Edgar Mint is a kid whose dead-pan, reflective voice belies the force with which he explodes on the page. Sometimes, some novels, you're introduced to characters who take awhile to brew with you. You have sit with them awhile, follow them around a bit before you feel like they're real. Edgar Mint, by contrast, is a kid who is so vivid, so authentic from the very first moment you meet him, that it's <em>you</em> who feels like a fake. <br /><br /><strong>3) The exercise of reading the Perfect Novel will, by default, render all other activities pointless, distracting and inconvenient in the extreme. Conversation will be scorned, commitments broken, sleep lost, meals missed and relationships with real-actual-people risked.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em><br />If it's not Edgar's voice that grabs you, it's his story. The opening line is: <em>"If I could tell you only one thing about my life it would be this: when I was 7 years old the mail man ran over my head."</em> If this was a normal novel, you would be able to put this book down after reading a few chapters and continue about your daily tasks like a normal person. But, since this is not a normal novel, but in fact the Perfect Novel, you should know that it will be IMPOSSIBLE to put this book down until every last footnote, dedication and page number has been read, nay, inhaled. <br /><br /><strong>4) The Perfect Novel always elicit some form of extreme emotion accompanied by its associated outward manifestation, i.e.: Happiness = laughter; tears = snot; anger = book tossing. Sometimes all at once.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em><br />Edgar Mint's life involves a series of unfortunate if not downright tragic turns that it's hard to comprehend why it is that you find yourself laughing so heartily so frequently. But then all of a sudden (because it's the Perfect Novel with the Perfect Story Arc), you unearth the mystery of Edgar's near-death experience and find the laughter has all but evaporated, leaving in its place an enormous weight of sadness that can only, inevitably, result in snot.<br /><br /><strong>5) The Perfect Novel may lead to hefty library fines.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em> <br />I can neither confirm nor deny whether I have yet returned Edgar Mint to the library after I borrowed it in January.<br /><br /><strong>6) The Perfect Novel is not a book, but a friend.</strong><br /><br /><em>Case in point</em> <br />I still cruise by the bookshelf every now and again to say Hi to Edgar Mint, (who may or may not be on my bookshelf) and to flip through the dog-eared pages to re-read a few of my favourite passages. Because friendships, like the Perfect Novel, are forever.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10635929640226233901">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
