<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_90011860">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90011860" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2690037-mimi" class="left imgcol" title="Mimi"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1363657977p2/2690037.jpg" alt="Mimi" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/90011860?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 16, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2690037-mimi" class="user" title="Mimi">Mimi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer90011860">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4540857464004527458">I started this story on the Amtrak trip back from Montreal, finishing  of the book on that 10 hour journey. The rest took me one more day. Couldn’t put it down!<br /><br />This is a 5-star tale of Edgar Mint, a young half-Apache whose earliest memory is waking up from a coma which resulted from the mailman running over his head when he was 7-years-old. Abandoned by his drunken mother and left-for-dead by all he knew, Edgar was an orphan left to fend for himself in the worlds of the substandard St. Divine ho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4540857464004527458">I started this story on the Amtrak trip back from Montreal, finishing ¾ of the book on that 10 hour journey. The rest took me one more day. Couldn’t put it down!<br /><br />This is a 5-star tale of Edgar Mint, a young half-Apache whose earliest memory is waking up from a coma which resulted from the mailman running over his head when he was 7-years-old. Abandoned by his drunken mother and left-for-dead by all he knew, Edgar was an orphan left to fend for himself in the worlds of the substandard St. Divine hospital, the horrid Willie Sherman Native American Boarding School, and his bizarrely dysfunctional Mormon foster family (the Madsens). Through all his trials and tribulations, Edgar never succumbs to hopelessness or despair, continuing on his quest to find the mailman who ran over his head simply to let him know that he was alive and well. The force drawing him to the mailman was beyond anything that Edgar (or I) would have ever imagined!<br /><br />This was a most amazing odyssey, with character both lovable and hateful…prose poetic, realistic and sometimes even gross! Political correctness was replaced by reality as this tale was told from a half-Apache’s perspective. <br /><br />I was astounded at how Mr. Udall created a narrative where answers were revealed and gaps were filled so completely and it all made so much sense at the end. Heartbreaking and heartwarming at the same time!<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4540857464004527458">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer453698930">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7106035685258879977">For a week I have been debating what to rate this. 4 or 5? 4.5? No, that isn't an option. 4 or 5? I'm going with five. <br /><br />What I LOVED about this book is it's honesty. This isn't an autobiography, but there is stuff in this book that couldn't be written without personal experience. I feel the author's vulnerability through his characters. My heart was open wide during the whole read. <br /><br />There is language in it, but the language is honest too. Swearing was used in situations, because the situation dema</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7106035685258879977">For a week I have been debating what to rate this. 4 or 5? 4.5? No, that isn't an option. 4 or 5? I'm going with five. <br /><br />What I LOVED about this book is it's honesty. This isn't an autobiography, but there is stuff in this book that couldn't be written without personal experience. I feel the author's vulnerability through his characters. My heart was open wide during the whole read. <br /><br />There is language in it, but the language is honest too. Swearing was used in situations, because the situation demanded it. It wouldn't be believable otherwise. Meeting the missionaries was also honest. The author did not shy away from talking about spiritual experiences or feelings. Even when Edgar made certain decisions that were against what he was taught there was unflinching honesty. <br /><br />I can't say enough about the characters. I knew them, I felt them, I could practically smell them. They were so different from each other. They were so distinct, each one of them. <br /><br />Was the book flawed? Yes. It went long in parts. I did contemplate suspending the read because of the language. But it was beautiful.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer177862991">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13964911095014731327">I'd probably give this 3.5 stars, but it wasn't quite enough to convince me to round up to 4 (so I guess I should say 3.4 starts to keep things consistent mathematically). I read this book because of how much I enjoyed <a title="The Lonely Polygamist by Brady Udall" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6944566.The_Lonely_Polygamist">The Lonely Polygamist</a>. There were elements of Udall's that I enjoyed in that book present in this one, but it didn't quite touch the same nerve.<br /><br />Early on this book reminded me of one of my favorites - <a title="A Prayer for Owen Meany by John Irving" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4473.A_Prayer_for_Owen_Meany">A Prayer for Owen Meany</a> - and that might have been a downfall before I even got g</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13964911095014731327">I'd probably give this 3.5 stars, but it wasn't quite enough to convince me to round up to 4 (so I guess I should say 3.4 starts to keep things consistent mathematically). I read this book because of how much I enjoyed <a title="The Lonely Polygamist by Brady Udall" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6944566.The_Lonely_Polygamist">The Lonely Polygamist</a>. There were elements of Udall's that I enjoyed in that book present in this one, but it didn't quite touch the same nerve.<br /><br />Early on this book reminded me of one of my favorites - <a title="A Prayer for Owen Meany by John Irving" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4473.A_Prayer_for_Owen_Meany">A Prayer for Owen Meany</a> - and that might have been a downfall before I even got going too far in. The narrator/protagonist is a seemingly miracle child, Edgar Mint. I did enjoy reading through the trajectory of his life...I don't want to give anything away...but there were parts that seemed to drag. Although in retrospect, maybe they were supposed to. Time was certainly a drag for Edgar in those times. <br /><br />Overall, this was a good read. If you only have time for one Udall, however, you should probably pick <a title="The Lonely Polygamist by Brady Udall" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6944566.The_Lonely_Polygamist">The Lonely Polygamist</a>.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer609489072">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2710853755049883074">It was really hard for me to rate this book. Truth be told, I would have rated the first two thirds a "3," and the final third a "4." I'm going with the "4" because that is where it ended. The story is launched by a horrible and absurdly dark event. In the first two thirds of the book, Brady leans too heavily on absurdity and pathetic and down-and-out characters to carry the story. This didn't work for me. I was sometimes bored and sometimes horrified, but never fully engaged by the story. It wa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2710853755049883074">It was really hard for me to rate this book. Truth be told, I would have rated the first two thirds a "3," and the final third a "4." I'm going with the "4" because that is where it ended. The story is launched by a horrible and absurdly dark event. In the first two thirds of the book, Brady leans too heavily on absurdity and pathetic and down-and-out characters to carry the story. This didn't work for me. I was sometimes bored and sometimes horrified, but never fully engaged by the story. It was the last third of the story that finally pulled me in. I felt like Brady took a big jump as a writer, and all of a sudden the absurdity and pathetic characters were used more effectively to make the story engaging and richer. It was in this third part where I really started to care about the characters, and where Brady's writing took on the emotional perceptiveness I had previously associated with him. The last third of the book felt a lot more like his writing in The Lonely Polygamist, which I think is the better book. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2710853755049883074">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer521839349">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9755266583510199146">This is one of my new favorite books. It's not as easy as one might think, to pick up a book that is literally hard to pull away from, but this is one of those books. I read it on the bus commute to and from work, on my lunch hours and in the dark of night when my insomnia kicked in.<br />It begins much like a Sherman Alexie story...and builds from there.<br />A young Apache boy who, due to trauma, has no memory of his early years (and consequently, you don't either...there is no 'filler')and many eccentric</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9755266583510199146">This is one of my new favorite books. It's not as easy as one might think, to pick up a book that is literally hard to pull away from, but this is one of those books. I read it on the bus commute to and from work, on my lunch hours and in the dark of night when my insomnia kicked in.<br />It begins much like a Sherman Alexie story...and builds from there.<br />A young Apache boy who, due to trauma, has no memory of his early years (and consequently, you don't either...there is no 'filler')and many eccentric friends who fill the void...his journey through a rez school and experience with the LDS church lead him to question his purpose on earth and why he's really here.<br />I did not see the end of the story coming...none of the story is predictable, but the full circle that is reached by the end, makes this a fulfilling read. Definately worth your time.<br />Book Club material? Yes!<br /><br />Ahhh, parting note, I discovered that this author has two more books (which I'm adding to my reading list) AND he lives in my town.<br />How awesome is that? :)</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_667773315">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/667773315" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11165434-sara-warner" class="left imgcol" title="Sara Warner"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1346763105p2/11165434.jpg" alt="Sara Warner" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/667773315?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 22, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11165434-sara-warner" class="user" title="Sara Warner">Sara Warner</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer667773315">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14244099663604770994">The Miracle of Writing<br /><br />It feels like there isn't a whole lot to say about <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint.</em> I took a long time reading it--no page turner, this. Instead, a layering up of the confusion and violence and searching that make up the truth of childhood, keenly wrought, with a restraint rare and beautiful to find in a writer.<br /><br />Brady Udall has a way with the everyday--the quiet, behind-the-scenes horrors that are the stunning landscape of life--bullies, family disfunction, sexual angst, mis-</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14244099663604770994">The Miracle of Writing<br /><br />It feels like there isn't a whole lot to say about <em>The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint.</em> I took a long time reading it--no page turner, this. Instead, a layering up of the confusion and violence and searching that make up the truth of childhood, keenly wrought, with a restraint rare and beautiful to find in a writer.<br /><br />Brady Udall has a way with the everyday--the quiet, behind-the-scenes horrors that are the stunning landscape of life--bullies, family disfunction, sexual angst, mis-socialization, death, addiction, betrayal. I'm tempted to say that a major ingredient of his effectiveness is this fairly flat, undramatic delivery of pretty high octane scenes. But really that would be wrong. Instead, maybe what we feel in this clean, strong prose is the balance of a narrator whose humanity holds steady in test after test. It's not that Edgar is heroic, not in the sense of invincible. For he flounders and fails in the maelstrom of life as much as anyone. I kept wondering as I read this story how people like Edgar have such an unerring moral compass when all around them are the hideous wrecks of humanity that perpetrate little more than evil upon evil. And maybe it has to do with Edgar's writing--the honest witnessing of everything that unfolds, an integrity that becomes his survival. Not allegiance to a particular code or dictum, not a faith. Just unflinching observation of what is. Which turns out to be not just hideous.<br /><br />There is something about this character, this narrator, this prose that embodies a restraint that emerges as beauty and human dignity. The days that build up a life, like pages of a book, turning and turning, seemingly incomplete in their isolation, becoming meaningful in their telling, are what Udall shows us here so masterfully. I didn't laugh out loud. I didn't cry. I didn't stay up to all hours, unable to put it down. Instead, I came to the last page of this story with a feeling of gratefulness that, in the midst of this flailing, stumbling gambol down the midway, I had just witnessed a little miracle.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14244099663604770994">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/427611-karen-a" class="left imgcol" title="Karen A."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347479457p2/427611.jpg" alt="Karen A." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/147794496?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 02, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/427611-karen-a" class="user" title="Karen A.">Karen A.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/427611-karen-a?shelf=literary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer147794496">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16935723241377287147">How I wanted this one to go on. I was very sorry to let Edgar go. Edgar, a half breed, whose head is crushed by a mail truck at the young age of seven, is one of the most compelling innocents to come along for some time. I would put this character up with Pip. Again, Mr. Udall, uses the American West, specifically Arizona and Utah, as his back drop. But really it is his intimate knowledge of the nooks and crannies of this deceptively empty desert that serves as an unforgiving shaper of little Ed</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16935723241377287147">How I wanted this one to go on. I was very sorry to let Edgar go. Edgar, a half breed, whose head is crushed by a mail truck at the young age of seven, is one of the most compelling innocents to come along for some time. I would put this character up with Pip. Again, Mr. Udall, uses the American West, specifically Arizona and Utah, as his back drop. But really it is his intimate knowledge of the nooks and crannies of this deceptively empty desert that serves as an unforgiving shaper of little Edgar’s life. The description and telling of Edgar’s life at Willie Sherman’s an Indian School for orphans should stir many a memory of institutional learning and while most experiences will not be as ‘savage’ as Edgar’s most will recognize the harsh power structure and subsequent injustices. The fact that this school is so remote from civilization makes it even more terrifying. Ultimately this is a book of hope and that the hope lives in the character of Edgar who steals and lies his way through his young life just to survive is compelling. Equally compelling the author’s light humorous touch with such dark themes. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16935723241377287147">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('7GWZmcSA5zhZ3Z2rRY/opg/dTHteUb5pEga8PYGaQW6JsaZKNlLzwtUF6RA77g4URiqPYheM+cN4D7mvTQ+3Kw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

In Austin, Texas, brassy Celia Mills, a once-vibrant librarian, mourns her murdered husband.\n\nOver the course of the summer, fate pushes these eerily recognizable women together, culminating in a revelation of the possibility of faith, the responsibility of friendship, and the value of life. Sleep Toward Heaven is a luminous story of murder and desire, solitude and grace -- a rare literary page-turner where redemption seems perpetually within arm\'s reach.&lt;\/span&gt;\n  &lt;a It was, however, a period when those who argued that a British empire would be a disaster for liberty were eventually squashed by imperialists, just as those who railed against mindless materialism were in the end rolled over by industrialists and the promoters of luxury goods. The Making of Victorian Values reveals an era when people were obsessed with the need to appear authentic, and yet forever had doubts about who was and who wasn\'t-concerns familiar to the &amp;quot;me&amp;quot; age we know so well.  Wilson begins with the libertine spirit inspired by Byron, Shelley, and the Romantics; he ends with the rise and eventual victory of stolid middle-class values. The result is a radical tour de force, a brilliant reworking of the pre-Victorian age. Once portrayed by Paul Johnson in his bestselling The Birth of the Modern as the years when virtue finally trumped corruption, Wilson reveals a far more compelling story-and a more engrossing and scandalous one, too. It is a story about hypochondriacs and cranks, killjoys and dandies, rakes and priests, advocates of free-speech and those against it-people who were made awe struck by Britain\'s emerging role as the economic and political powerhouse of the world, but who were also deeply anxious about the responsibilities a vast empire might require.  Wilson is heir to the great radical historians of the twentieth century, E. J. Hobsbawm and E. P. Thompson, among them. He brushes aside scholarly politesse and refuses to join in unnecessary academic point-settling, and his invigorating literary abilities will win many admirers who would otherwise know this history     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/97389.Brady_Udall">About Brady Udall</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/97389.Brady_Udall"><img alt="97389" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1314995234p2/97389.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer4949832682922263011">Brady Udall grew up in a large Mormon family in Arizona, where he worked on his grandfather's farm. He graduated from Brigham Young University and later attended the Iowa Writers' Workshop. <br /><br />He was formerly a faculty member of Franklin &amp; Marshall College starting in 1998, then Southern Illinois University, and now teaches writing at Boise State University. <br /><br />A collection of his short stories titl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4949832682922263011">Brady Udall grew up in a large Mormon family in Arizona, where he worked on his grandfather's farm. He graduated from Brigham Young University and later attended the Iowa Writers' Workshop. <br /><br />He was formerly a faculty member of Franklin &amp; Marshall College starting in 1998, then Southern Illinois University, and now teaches writing at Boise State University. <br /><br />A collection of his short stories titled Letting Loose the Hounds was published in 1998 and his debut novel The Miracle Life of Edgar Mint was published in 2001. The Lonely Polygamist was published in May 2010.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4949832682922263011">...more</a>

    </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/963426-the-miracle-life-of-edgar-mint">Quotes from The Miracle Life ...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“There was one difference I would come to realize, between white kids and Indians. Among white kids there are tattletales everywhere. Indians? An Indian wouldn't tattle to save his own mother. Indians, over the years, have learned the value of keeping their mouths shut.”</span>
            <nobr>
